## Transcript

- [00:00] American psychologist, and professor of psychiatry, Kay Redfield Jamison, is one of the world's
- [00:04] foremost authorities on bipolar disorder. She's spent her career researching, lecturing,
- [00:09] and writing seminal books on the condition.
- [00:11] A condition that she also happens to have had her entire adult life.
- [00:15] In her memoir, "An Unquiet Mind," Jamison details what it really means to be bipolar.
- [00:20] She writes of not sleeping for days on end, of feeling long periods of euphoria, and filling
- [00:25] whole notebooks with her racing thoughts and grandiose ideas.
- [00:29] While in these manic states, she experienced a tremendously inflated sense of self-esteem
- [00:33] and did impulsive things that felt good at the time but had painful consequences, like
- [00:37] going on lavish shopping sprees, engaging in promiscuous behavior, racking up credit
- [00:42] card debt, and emptying her bank accounts.
- [00:44] But these episodes were followed by emotional crashes: Crippling bouts of depression that
- [00:48] sent her into a suicidal spiral. At the age of 28, Jamison tried to kill herself by taking
- [00:53] an overdose of Lithium, lapsed into a coma, but thankfully emerged from it determined
- [00:57] to find help through medication and therapy.
- [00:59] Through her research and writing, Dr. Jamison has pioneered our understanding of bipolar
- [01:03] disorder, depression, and the nexus of mental struggles that we now think of as mood disorders.
- [01:08] And she's probably one of the best ambassadors we have for all those people who live successful,
- [01:13] productive lives with mental illness.
- [01:15] Just like the anxiety disorders we talked about last time, mood disorders are misunderstood.
- [01:19] They're diluted by depictions of depression as something that can be treated with one
- [01:23] day at a spa or descriptions of people as manic depressive just because they were sad
- [01:27] yesterday and aren't today.
- [01:29] As students of psychology, our job is to understand what mood disorders really are, how they manifest
- [01:33] themselves, and what might cause them. And as you probably guessed, this can be pretty
- [01:37] tough terrain to explore. These disorders can take people from terrifying highs to pits
- [01:42] of despair that seem all but bottomless.
- [01:44] But! In between there's what Jamison has called,  "A rich, imaginative life"
- [01:48] -- all made possible by your moods.
- [02:01] We've been talking a lot about terms and concepts that mean something different than what you
- [02:03] think they mean, but this time, the term "Mood" is not one of those.
- [02:07] In a psychological context, moods are pretty much exactly what you think they are: Emotional
- [02:11] states that are even more subjective and harder to define than the emotions themselves.
- [02:16] And while psychologists have defined about 10 basic emotions, moods tend to fall
- [02:20] into two broadly and infinitely variable categories. You got the good moods and the bad moods.
- [02:26] Probably the most important distinction between emotion and mood is that moods are long-term
- [02:31] emotional states rather than discreet, fleeting feelings.
- [02:34] And "mood-disorders," which are characterized by emotional extremes and challenges in regulating
- [02:39] mood tend to be longer-term disturbances.
- [02:42] These include depressive disorders, typified by prolonged hopelessness and lethargy, and
- [02:46] bipolar disorders, the most prominent of which involve alternating between depression and mania.
- [02:50] Depression has been called the common cold of psychological disorders. Which is not to
- [02:54] say that it isn't serious, but it's common and it's pervasive and it's the top reason
- [02:58] people seek out mental health help.
- [03:00] We've all felt down before, obviously, often in response to a specific loss: a breakup
- [03:05] or a lost job or the death of a loved one.
- [03:07] And the fact is, you probably should feel bad at times like those. It can actually be
- [03:11] good for a mind and body to slow down, to help digest losses that you experience, but
- [03:16] in general, sadness is temporary. It's when sadness and grief extend beyond the generally
- [03:20] accepted social norms, or plunge into a depth that causes serious dysfunction that you find
- [03:25] yourself in the territory of depressive disorders.
- [03:28] The DSM-5, our handy (if super flawed) user's guide to psychological disorders officially
- [03:32] diagnoses a major depressive disorder when a patient has experienced at least five signs
- [03:37] of depression for more than two weeks.
- [03:39] These symptoms include not just depressed mood, but also significant weight or appetite
- [03:44] loss or gain, too much or too little sleep, decreased interest in activities, feeling
- [03:48] worthless, fatigued, or lethargic, difficulty concentrating or making decisions, and recurrent
- [03:53] thoughts of death or suicide.
- [03:55] So while everyone experiences sadness, depression is a physiological as well as psychological
- [04:00] illness. It messes with your sleep, and appetite, and energy, and neurotransmitter levels, all
- [04:05] interfering with the way your body runs itself.
- [04:07] Plus in keeping with our definition of psychological disorders, to be considered a true disorder
- [04:11] this behavior needs to cause the person or others around them prolonged distress - the
- [04:16] feeling that something is really wrong.
- [04:19] Just as a person with a severe, generalized anxiety disorder may never want to leave the
- [04:22] house, a clinically depressed person often feels so hopeless and overwhelmed that they
- [04:26] have trouble living a normal life. And unlike the bipolar disorders, the depressive disorders
- [04:30] tend to be all lows.
- [04:31] You've probably heard of manic depression. It's the outdated term for bipolar disorders.
- [04:36] These include those classic dark lows of depression, but also bouts of the opposite - of extreme
- [04:41] mania in more severe cases. Someone suffering from a bipolar disorder may flip back and
- [04:45] forth between normal and depressive and manic phases within a single day or week or month.
- [04:50] And a true manic episode doesn't just mean being energetic or happy, it's a period of
- [04:54] intense, restless, but often optimistic hyperactivity in which your estimation of yourself and your
- [05:00] abilities and your ideas can often get skewed. Like, really, REALLY skewed.
- [05:05] Some patients experience mania only rarely, but when they do, it can be destructive. Kay
- [05:09] Jamison has testified to that.
- [05:11] Once during a manic episode, she bought up a drug store's entire supply of snake-bite
- [05:15] kits, convinced of an imminent attack of rattlesnakes that only she knew was coming.
- [05:19] In another, she purchased 20 books by the Penguin Publishing House because she said,
- [05:24] "It could be nice if the penguins could form a colony."
- [05:27] In other words, bad judgment is common. And it can get worse.
- [05:31] Full blown manic episodes often end up in psychiatric hospitalization, since the risk
- [05:35] to self or others can become severe. When the highs eventually end, they're often followed
- [05:40] by dark periods of depression. When left untreated, suicide or suicide attempts are common, another
- [05:45] element of the disorder that Jamison herself can attest to.
- [05:48] Like so many things in psychology, the cause of mood disorders is often a combination of
- [05:52] biological, genetic, psychological, and environmental factors. We know, for example, that mood disorders
- [05:57] run in families - genes matter. And you're more likely to experience a bipolar or depressive
- [06:03] disorder if you have parents or siblings who suffer from them.
- [06:05] Studies have of identical twins show that if one twin has a bipolar disorder, that the
- [06:09] other has a seven in ten chance of also being diagnosed, regardless of whether they were
- [06:13] raised together or apart.
- [06:15] And while a stressful life can't give you bipolar disorder, it could trigger a manic
- [06:20] or depressive episode in someone with a pre-existing condition. Or start a descent into a major
- [06:24] depressive episode in someone who never before had experienced depression. In other words,
- [06:27] a person who loses a loved one could go from sad to depressed or slide into a bipolar episode,
- [06:32] but it couldn't cause them to have the disorder to begin with.
- [06:35] In the case of depressive disorders, for most people, after weeks, months, or even years,
- [06:40] their depression can end, hopefully with the return to baseline healthy functioning.
- [06:44] World-wide, women tend to be diagnosed with major depression more often than men, but
- [06:48] many psychologists think this is simply because women tend to seek treatment more. It's also
- [06:51] possible that depression in men tends to manifest itself more in terms of anger and aggression,
- [06:56] than as sadness and hopelessness.
- [06:57] This is just an example of how depression is much more than just being sad and that
- [07:01] the characteristic lack of purpose and helplessness can manifest itself in a lot of different ways.
- [07:05] Looking at mood disorders from a neurological perspective, we see that depressed, manic,
- [07:09] and average brains show very different brain activity in neural imaging scans. As you might
- [07:14] expect, a brain in a depressed state slows down. While a brain in a manic state shows
- [07:18] a lot of increased activity, making it hard for that person to calm down or focus or sleep.
- [07:23] Our brain's neurotransmitter chemistry also changes with these different states. For example,
- [07:27] norepinephrine, which usually increases arousal and focus, is severely lacking in depressed
- [07:31] brains, but kind of off the charts during manic episodes. In fact, drugs that seek to
- [07:36] reduce mania in part do it by reducing norepinephrine levels. You may have also heard about how
- [07:41] low serotonin levels correlate with depressive states. Exercise, like jogging or break dancing
- [07:47] or whatever, increases serotonin levels, which is one reason exercise is often recommended
- [07:51] to combat depression. And most medications designed to treat depression seem to work
- [07:55] by raising serotonin or norepinephrine levels.
- [07:58] And of course there's yet another way to look at things. The social-cognitive perspective
- [08:02] examines how our thinking and behavior influence depression.
- [08:05] People with depression often view bad events through an internal lens or mind set that
- [08:09] influences how they're interpreted. And how you explain events to yourself, in a negative
- [08:14] or positive way, can really effect how you recover from them - or don't.
- [08:18] Say you were humiliated in the lunch room when someone tripped you and chicken soup
- [08:21] flew all over the place, and you sat down on a brownie, and it was just a bad day. A
- [08:25] depressive mind might immediately start thinking that the humiliation will last forever, that
- [08:29] no one will ever let you live it down, that it's somehow your own fault, and you can't
- [08:33] ever do anything right.
- [08:34] That negative thinking, learned helplessness, self-blame, and over-thinking can feed off
- [08:39] itself and basically smother the joy out of the brain, eventually creating a vicious self-fulfilling
- [08:44] cycle of negative thinking.
- [08:46] The good news is that the cycle can be broken by getting help from a professional, turning
- [08:49] your attention outward, doing more fun things, and maybe even moving to a different environment.
- [08:54] But again, that social-cognitive prospective is just part of a much bigger puzzle. Positive
- [08:58] thinking is important, but it's often inadequate on its own own when up against genetic or neurological factors.
- [09:03] So mood disorders are complicated conditions and rarely are they eliminated with a single
- [09:07] cure. Instead, they're often things you just live with. And as Dr. Jamison has shown us,
- [09:12] you can live well.
- [09:14] Today we talked about what mood disorders are, as well as what they aren't. You learned
- [09:18] about the symptoms of depressive and bipolar disorders, and the possible biological, genetic,
- [09:22] environmental, and social-cognitive causes of mood disorders.
- [09:25] Thank you for watching this episode, which was brought to you by Marshall Scott and
- [09:29] crediblefind.com. Thank you so much to all of you that have supported us! To find out
- [09:33] how you can become a sponsor or supporter, just go to suppable.com/crashcourse.

- [09:41] is Dr. Ranjit Bhagwat. Our director and editor is Nicholas Jenkins. The script supervisor
- [09:45] is Michael Aranda who is also our sound designer. And the graphics team is Thought Cafe.
